[1] Chapter One
[2] Old Lanscomb stumbled from one room to another, pulling the blinds open one by one. His eyes, filled with tears, were surrounded by wrinkles, and he occasionally glanced out the window.
[3] They should be almost back from the funeral. He picked up his pace slightly, as there were too many windows.
[4] Endebury Manor is a Gothic-style colossal building built in the Victorian era. Each room features heavy silk or velvet curtains that have slightly faded. Some walls still display old silk. The elderly butler, Lanscombe, enters the living room with a greenish hue and examines the portrait hanging above the fireplace mantel, which depicts Cornelius Abernathy, for whom Endebury Manor was constructed. His brown beard protrudes aggressively forward, with his hand resting on a globe, making it unclear whether the composition was due to his own wishes or the painter's use of symbolism.
[5] What a formidable gentleman! Old Lanscombe often thought so, and was glad he had never crossed paths with him. Mr. Richard was the true gentleman in his mind, a good host, and the doctor had been treating him for some time, yet he had suddenly passed away. Alas, the death of Mortimer, Jr. had been too much for him to bear, and he had never fully recovered from his grief. The old man shook his head and hurried into the adjoining white bedroom. It was terrible, a real tragedy. Such a young, vigorous gentleman, so full of life, you never would have expected something like that to happen to him. Poor man, so very unfortunate. Gordon, Jr. had died in the war again. One blow after another, that's how things are these days. It's just too much for the master to bear. However, only a week ago, he seemed perfectly healthy.
[6] The third casement window in the white bedroom couldn't be raised any further; it stuck at the very beginning of the attempt. The spring was on its last legs — it must be the issue here — these casement windows were all too old, just like the other things in this house, and this old stuff couldn't be repaired anymore. "Too old," they would always say, shaking their heads in contempt — as if there was nothing better than old stuff! He could clearly tell these people! Half of the new stuff was flimsy and cheap — they'd be finished as soon as they were brought home. The materials were subpar, and the craftsmanship was even worse. Yes, he could clearly tell them.
[7] It seems there's no other way but to get a ladder. In recent years, he has disliked climbing ladders, always making him dizzy. Never mind, it should be fine as it is. The bedroom window is not on the front of the house, so people driving back from the funeral should not be able to see it – and this bedroom seems to have never been used before. It's a lady's boudoir, and Enderby hasn't had a lady for a long time. Mr. Mortimer hasn't married, which is a pity. He always goes fishing in Norway, hunting in Scotland, or skiing in Switzerland, but never thinks of marrying a virtuous and gentle lady to settle down and enjoy a house full of children playing around. It's been a long time since children have been seen in this house.
[8] Lancing's mind vividly recalls a period of the past - much clearer than the memories of the past twenty years, which are vague and mixed. People come and go, and he has a hard time remembering them. But the memories of those old times are crystal clear.
[9] For his younger siblings, Mr. Richard was more like a father than an older brother. At the age of twenty-four, after the death of their father, he took over the family business, working diligently and consistently to maintain the luxurious lifestyle for the family. The sisters and brothers grew up together, forming a very harmonious household. Of course, there were occasional disagreements, and the female governesses suffered greatly as a result! They were all timid individuals, and Mr. Lunscombe always looked down on the governesses. The sisters were very energetic, especially Miss Geraldine. There was also Miss Cora, although she was much younger. Now, Mr. Leigh has passed away; Miss Laura has also passed away; Mr. Timothy is consumed by grief and has become a recluse; Miss Geraldine died abroad; Mr. Gordon was killed in the war; Mr. Richard, the eldest, turned out to be the strongest among the siblings, although not the longest-lived, as Mr. Timothy is still alive; and Miss Cora married an obnoxious artist. Mr. Lunscombe has not seen her in twenty-five years, and she left as a young and beautiful woman, now almost unrecognizable, short and stout, dressed in affected attire, pretending to be an artist! Her husband is French, or of French descent – marrying such a man can only lead to disaster! However, Miss Cora has always been somewhat – childish, to put it kindly, innocent. Every family has its own character like this.
[10] She remembers him. "Oh, it's Lancelot!" She seems pleased to see him. Ah, they all used to like him, sneaking into the pantry during dinner parties and he would bring out dishes of jelly and cream pudding for them. They all knew the old Lancelot, but now hardly anyone remembers who he is. The younger generation, he thinks, doesn't even know who he is; they only know him as the old butler who has served this house for many years. As they come to the funeral, he thinks to himself, they are all strangers—a bunch of annoying strangers!
[11] This does not include Leah's wife - she is different. After getting married to Leah's husband, the couple would occasionally come to visit. Leah's wife, she is a lady - a true lady. She is well-dressed, has an elegant hairstyle, and her actions are always in line with her social status. The host always likes her. Unfortunately, she and Leah's husband still do not have children…
[12] Lancelot snapped out of his reverie. There were still so many things to do; standing here reminiscing was pointless. The shutters downstairs were all open, so he should send Jenny upstairs to open the bedroom windows. He, Jenny, and the cook had returned from the church service, opened all the shutters, and prepared for lunch. Of course, it had to be a cold spread - ham, chicken, beef tongue, and salad, with desserts of lemon custard and apple pie. First, they would have hot soup - he should check on Margaret to make sure she was ready in a couple of minutes.
[13] Lancing quickened his pace, passing through the room. His eyes were inadvertently drawn to the portrait on the mantelpiece - a pair with the one hanging in the living room. The white silk garments and pearls were exquisitely painted, but the figure wearing them and the jewelry was obscured, taking away some of the radiance. She was a gentle, modest woman with rosebud lips, parted hair, and a calm demeanor. Mrs. Cornelia Abner West, the only thing worth mentioning about her was her name - Cora Lee.
[14] Since its inception over sixty years ago, the Kola Flour Mill and its affiliated Kola Shoe Company have been doing quite well. No one knows exactly what makes the Kola family's business so special – but the family's intriguing history has always piqued the public's curiosity. It is precisely because of this wealthy family business that the new Gothic palace, along with the surrounding several acres of gardens, was built. The Kola family also ensures that their seven children receive their regular payments, which is why Richard Abner, who passed away three days ago, was quite wealthy.

[16] Lancelot stuck his head into the kitchen and hurriedly urged her, only to be reprimanded by Muriel. The cook Muriel was very young, only twenty-seven, and she was a thorn in Lancelot's side because she did not meet his standards for a good cook. She did not show any respect for Lancelot's title, constantly calling this house an "ancient, gloomy tomb," and complaining about the kitchen being too large, with a washing area and a food storage area, saying that "it would take a whole day to walk from one end to the other." She had been in Enfield for two years, but had not resigned, partly because of the generous salary and partly because Mrs. Abernathy loved her excellent cooking skills. Jenny, an elderly maid, stood next to the kitchen counter, drinking tea. Although she often quarreled with Lancelot, she had always been on his side, fighting against Muriel and the younger generation.
[17] "It's beautiful, really," she poured a cup of tea and sniffed it elegantly, saying, "Nineteen cars, the church was packed. The pastor's prayer was beautiful, I think. Today is a great day for a funeral. Oh, poor Mr. Abernathy, there aren't many like him left in the world. No one disrespected him."
[18] The car horn sounded, followed by the sound of the car approaching. Mrs. Jax immediately put down her teacup and shouted, "They're here."
[19] Muriel turned on the gas stove, which had a large pot of cream chicken soup on it. The huge Victorian-era stove stood coldly by the side, like an altar commemorating the past.
[20] The cars pulled up one by one, and people in black clothes hesitated as they walked through the entrance hall and into the green living room. The fire in the steel fireplace burned fiercely, dispelling the chill of the autumn day and softening the solemn atmosphere of the funeral.
[21] Lancelot entered the room with a silver tray, serving sherry to the people in the living room.
[22] Mr. Entwistle, a senior partner of the prestigious Bollard-Entwistle firm, leaned against the fireplace to warm himself. He took a glass of sherry and looked around the room with the sharp-eyed gaze of a lawyer. Not everyone present was an acquaintance, so it was necessary to get to know them one by one. The introductions before the funeral had been hasty and perfunctory.
[23] Mr. Entwistle thought to himself that it was a pity that old Lancelot was getting slower with age - he wouldn't be surprised if he lived to be ninety. Yes, he had a generous pension, so he didn't have to worry about anything. A loyal servant, a rare breed in this day and age. Nowadays, it's all about temporary help and nannies. God help us! What a sad world. Maybe it's a good thing that poor Richard passed away early. There's nothing left in this world worth living for.
[24] For Mr. Entwistle, who was seventy-two years old this year, Richard Abney's passing at sixty-eight was too early. Mr. Entwistle had retired two years ago, but as Richard Abney's executor, he made the long journey north out of respect for his old client and old friend.
[25] As he reviewed the terms of the will, he quietly examined the family.
[26] Mrs. Leigh - Helen, of course, he was very familiar with. She was a very charming lady whom he liked and respected. His approving gaze fell upon her. She stood by the window, and black suited her well. She maintained her figure. He liked her angular face, her gray hair swept back from her temples, and her sapphire-blue eyes, still as blue as ever. Helen was probably fifty-one or fifty-two years old, he thought. It was strange that she hadn't remarried after Leigh's death. She was a very attractive woman. Ah, but the couple was very loving.
[27] His gaze shifted to Mrs. Timothy. He didn't know her very well. Black didn't suit her - she wore a country tweed jacket, showing her capable nature. She had always been a faithful wife to Timothy. She took care of his health and worried about his small affairs - perhaps a bit too much. Was Timothy really sick? In Mr. Entwistle's opinion, it was just hypochondria. Richard Abnercise had also thought so. "He was weak-hearted when he was young," he had said in the past, "but I don't think he has any serious illness now." Ah, everyone has their own hobbies. Timothy's hobby was worrying about his health nonstop. Was Mrs. Timothy deceived? It should be impossible - but women never admit to being deceived. Timothy's life must have been comfortable. He was never stingy in spending.
[28] Mr. Entwistle's attention turned to George Crosfield, the son of Laura. Laura's husband was a respectable man who claimed to be a stockbroker. George worked in a law firm - not a famous one. He was handsome, but he seemed cunning. His life must have been tight. Laura was a complete fool when it came to investing, and when she died five years ago, she had almost nothing left. She was a beautiful and romantic girl, but she had no idea about finance.
[29] Mr. Entwistle shifted his gaze from George Crosfield. Who were the two girls? Ah, yes, the one staring at the peacock feather on the table was Rosamond, the daughter of Geraldine. She was a pretty girl, indeed beautiful - with a silly, foolish expression. She was in the entertainment industry, performing in a rotating repertory company, and married an actor - a very handsome man. "And very clear about his own advantages," Mr. Entwistle thought, and he didn't like people in the entertainment industry. "I don't know where he came from or what his background is."
[30] Mr. Entwistle shifted his gaze from Rosamond. The two girls were who? Ah, yes, the one staring at the peacock feather on the table was Rosamond, the daughter of Geraldine. She was a pretty girl, indeed beautiful - with a silly, foolish expression. She was in the entertainment industry, performing in a rotating repertory company, and married an actor - a very handsome man. "And very clear about his own advantages," Mr. Entwistle thought, and he didn't like people in the entertainment industry. "
[31] He looked at Michael Shan with disdain, watching the wild allure of his flowing golden hair.
[32] Another girl is Susan, Gordon's daughter, who would be far superior on the stage if she were to appear. She is more distinctive, perhaps a little too so in everyday life. She stands close to him, and Mr. Entwistle observes her covertly. Dark hair, light brown – almost golden – eyes, a melancholy and captivating mouth. Standing beside her is her newlywed husband – apparently a pharmacy assistant, as far as he knows. Truly, a pharmacy assistant! In Mr. Entwistle's opinion, a girl should never marry someone who stands behind a counter serving others. However, of course, in this day and age, they can marry anyone! This young man is unremarkable in appearance, with a pale complexion, light brown hair, and seems quite uncomfortable. Mr. Entwistle charitably attributes this to his seeing so many relatives of his wife, and being overly tense.
[33] His last subject of observation was Cora Lanscourne. It was only fair to leave her to the end, Cora being Richard's youngest sister and something of an outsider in the family - her mother was fifty when she was born. The gentle woman did not survive the tenth birth in the family - the other three children had died young. Poor Cora! A lifetime of embarrassment, tall and clumsy, and prone to saying inappropriate things. Her brothers and sisters had always been kind to her, trying to hide her shortcomings and make up for her mistakes. No one had expected Cora to marry - she was never a particularly attractive girl, but she always boldly approached young men, who were eager to avoid her. Enthwistle smiled as he thought of this, and now it was time to talk about Lanscourne - Pierre Lanscourne, with half French blood, whom Cora had met at an art school studying watercolor flower painting, and for some reason changed to a life guidance course, where she met Pierre Lanscourne and then came home to announce her intention to marry him. Richard Abney was vehemently opposed - he did not like this Pierre Lanscourne and suspected that the young man only wanted to marry a rich woman. While he was investigating Lanscourne's background, Cora and the man eloped and married. For most of their married life, they lived in Brittany and Cornwall, as well as some of the usual places where painters reside. As a painter, Lanscourne was terrible, and as a man, he was no better, but Cora was devoted to him and always willing to forgive her family's attitude towards her husband. Richard generously provided Cora with some money, and Enthwistle believed that it was thanks to this money that they were able to maintain their lifestyle. He even suspected that Lanscourne had ever earned a penny. He had been dead for twelve years, or more, Enthwistle thought, and now his widow stood here, her body bulging like a cushion, wrapped in a fine black dress and wearing a black jade necklace, back to her childhood home, poking around and calling out excitedly when she remembered something from her childhood. She didn't bother pretending to be sad about her eldest brother's death, but Enthwistle immediately thought that Cora never pretended.
[34] Re-entering the living room, Lanscombe spoke in a well-modulated low voice, "Lunch is ready."
[35] Chapter Two
[36] After enjoying a delicious chicken soup, accompanied by Chablis, and sampling various exquisite cold dishes, the somber atmosphere of the funeral was somewhat alleviated. No one in the room truly mourned the death of Richard Abney, as they were not particularly close to him. The display of grief was only due to a suitable degree of respect and self-control - except for Cora, who seemed to enjoy it all. Now that the necessary rituals had been completed, it was time to return to normal conversation. Mr. Entwistle approved of this attitude. He had attended many funerals and knew how to manage the pace of the funeral.
[37] After lunch, Lanscour led everyone to the study for coffee. This was a thoughtful move on his part. It was time to discuss the matter at hand - in other words, the will - and it was time to talk about it. The study was filled with bookshelves and heavy red velvet curtains, and the atmosphere was perfect for discussing this matter. After serving coffee to everyone, he quietly left the room and closed the door.
[38] After a few indifferent words, everyone looked tentatively at Mr. Entwistle. He immediately responded, glancing at his watch.
[39] "I have to catch the 3:30 train." He said.
[40] Everyone else seemed to have to catch the same train.
[41] "As you all know," Mr. Entwistle said, "I am the executor of Richard Abernathy's will -"
[42] His words were interrupted.
[43] "I didn't know," Cora Lanscombe said cheerfully, "Is it you? What did he leave me?"
[44] This was not the first time Mr. Entwistle felt that Cora spoke inappropriately.
[45] He silenced her with a look and continued speaking:
[46] "Just a year ago, Richard Abner West's will was very simple. Everything except part of his property was left to his son—Mortimer."
[47] "Poor Mortimer," Cora interjected, "polio is terrible!"
[48] "Mortimer's death was a tragic accident, sudden and devastating for Richard. It took him months to recover. I reminded him then that it would be best to make a new will."
[49] Moddy Abner West asked in a low voice:
[50] "What would have happened if he hadn't made a new will? Would all the estate have gone to Timothy—I mean, to his nearest relative?"
[51] Mr. Entwistle decided to give her a lesson on what constitutes a closest relative, thought better of it, and continued:
[52] "Richard took my advice and decided to make a new will. Then, before that, he wanted to get to know the younger generation better."
[53] "He wanted to see what he was getting into before deciding," Susan suddenly laughed, "first George, then Greg and me, then Rosamond and Michael."
[54] Greg Banks' thin face turned red, and he suddenly said:
[55] "I don't think you should say that, Susan, seeing what you're getting into before deciding is too much!"
[56] "But that's the truth, isn't it, Mr. Entwistle?"
[57] "What did he leave me?" Cora asked again.
[58] Mr. Entwistle cleared his throat and spoke coldly:
[59] "I am preparing to send a copy of the will to everyone here. If you wish, I can read it from beginning to end for you, but it is full of legal jargon that will be difficult for you to understand. In short, it is as follows: some small personal items and a real estate inheritance are left to Lancelot as a pension, and the rest of the assets - which are quite substantial - are divided into six equal parts. Four of these, after tax, are left to Timothy, Richard's brother, his nephew George Crosfield, his niece Susan Banks, and his niece Rosamond Shayne. The remaining two will be placed in a trust fund, the income of which will be owned by his brother Leo's widow Helen Abney and his sister Cora Lanscombe, who will enjoy this income for as long as they live. After their deaths, the income will be divided equally among the other four beneficiaries or their descendants."
[60] "That's wonderful!" Cora exclaimed, "A share of the income! How much will it be?"
[61] "I - er - can't give you an exact figure. Taxes, of course, will be heavy, and -"
[62] "Can't you give me an idea of how much?"
[63] Mr. Entwistle realized that he needed to give her a figure to satisfy her.
[64] "Probably around £3,000 to £4,000 per year."
[65] "Fantastic!" Cora said. "I can finally go to Capri."
[66] Mrs. Abney West gently spoke:
[67] "Richard is generous and kind. I'm grateful for his friendship."
[68] "He liked you a lot," said Mr. Entwistle. "Among his siblings, he liked Mr. Leigh the most, and after Mr. Leigh's passing, Mr. Richard appreciated your frequent visits to him."
[69] Mrs. Abney West regretfully said:
[70] "If I had known his illness was so severe—I visited him not long before he passed away, although I knew he was ill, I didn't expect it to be that serious."
[71] "It has been serious all along," Mr. Entwistle said, "He didn't want to mention it, and I believe no one expected him to pass away so quickly, even the doctors were surprised."
[72] "‘Died suddenly at home,’ that's what the newspaper said," Cora nodded, "But I was shocked when I heard the news."
[73] "It's hard to believe for all of us," Mrs. Abernathy said, "It was such a shock for Timothy. He kept saying, too sudden, too sudden."
[74] "He did keep the secret, didn't he?" Cora replied.
[75] Everyone in the room turned their gaze towards her, making her feel a bit uneasy.
[76] "I think you're all right," she added hastily, "quite correct. I mean - there's no good - bringing this up, making everyone unhappy. It's just something for us to know."
[77] Looking at her faces, she grew more and more bewildered.
[78] Mr. Entwistle leaned forward:
[79] "Honestly, Cora, I'm not quite sure what you mean."
[80] Cora Lanscourte widened her eyes and looked around the room at the family gathered there. She tilted her head to one side like a bird.
[81] "But he was murdered, wasn't he?" she said.
[82] Chapter Three
[83] Mr. Entwistle sat in a corner of the first-class carriage on the train to London, growing more and more uneasy as he recalled Cora Lanscombe's unusual words. Of course, Cora was a rather eccentric simpleton, and it had been noticed ever since she was a little girl that she would occasionally blurt out awkward truths. No, that wasn't quite right - the word "truths" was all wrong. It should be "awkward remarks" - that was much better.
[84] He remembered the scene after Cora had spoken her ominous words. So many shocked and condemning glances had been directed at her, and Cora had seemed to realize the gravity of her statement.
[85] "Goodness gracious, Cora!" exclaimed Maud. "George said, "My dear Aunt Cora." Someone else said, "What do you mean?"
[86] At that moment, Cora Lanscombe felt guilty and mortified, and she stammered out a series of broken sentences.
[87] "Oh, I'm so sorry - I didn't mean - oh, of course, I'm such an idiot, but I just heard him say it, so - oh, of course, I know it's not wrong, it's just that he died so suddenly - please forget what I said - I didn't mean to be so stupid - I know I always blurt out things."
[88] The uncomfortable atmosphere soon dissipated, and people began discussing practical matters regarding the disposal of Richard Abernathy's estate. The entire Entwistle estate and its contents would be auctioned off, Mr. Entwistle added.
[89] Cora's slip of the tongue was quickly forgotten. After all, she was always so naively embarrassing - if not outright deranged. She had no idea what she should or should not say. When she was young, it was merely considered "childish," and people would laugh it off, but if she was still speaking so candidly at nearly fifty, it was quite unacceptable. She would suddenly blurt out unwelcome truths - the word "truths" suddenly interrupted Mr. Entwistle's thoughts. Why were these words so unsettling? Of course, it was because Cora's off-the-cuff remarks always contained elements of embarrassment and awkwardness. They often felt awkward because they contained some degree of truth!
[90] Despite the matronly woman's bulky figure and the fact that she was now forty-nine, there were still some quirks of Cora's that Mr. Entwistle could recognize - whenever she uttered something distasteful, her head would tilt to the side like a bird, displaying a cheerful demeanor that conveyed her eagerness to hear a response. With this demeanor, Cora had once commented on the kitchen maid's figure: "Molly's belly is so big that she can't even reach the kitchen counter. She looks like she's pregnant for eight or nine months. I wonder why she's gotten so fat?"
[91] Cora was quickly silenced. The Abner family adhered to the strict Victorian upbringing. The kitchen maid did not appear the next day, and after some investigation, a gardener was ordered to marry her and provide her with a small cottage.
[92] It was a long time ago — but there was some truth to it...
[93] Mr. Entwistle further examined the reason for his unease. What was it about Cora's absurd remark that touched his subconscious? He extracted two sentences from her words, "But I only heard him say it —" and "It's just that he died so suddenly..."
[94] Mr. Entwistle began investigating the second sentence. Indeed, Richard's death could be considered sudden by conventional standards. He had discussed Richard's condition with both Richard and Richard's doctor, who candidly told him that, given Richard's current condition, he could not expect to live long, but if he took good care of himself, he should be able to live for two or even three more years. Perhaps even longer — although the likelihood was slim. Regardless, the doctor had not predicted a short-term death.
[95] It seemed that the doctor was wrong — but doctors had never claimed to have a firm grasp on every patient's reaction to illness, and they admitted as much. Some patients with no hope of recovery had recovered, while others who were doing well had suddenly worsened and died. The key was the patient's own vitality, their innate will to survive.
[96] Richard Abney was a robust and vigorous man, but he had lost the motivation to continue living.
[97] Six months ago, his only surviving son, Mortimer, had contracted polio and died within a week. His death was a shock to Richard. He was so strong, full of life, adventurous, and skilled in various sports. People always said he had never been sick a day in his life. He was about to get engaged to a charming young lady, and his father's future hopes were pinned on this beloved son of his.
[98] Tragedy struck. The future held no meaning for Richard Abney, only the pain of losing a son. One son had died shortly after birth, and the second had died without any offspring. He had no grandson. In fact, the Abney lineage was about to end, and who would inherit his wealth and take over his business?
[99] Mr. Entwistle knew that this worried Richard. His only surviving brother was a cripple, and the remaining options were the younger generation. The lawyer surmised that Richard had plans, though he did not say so, to choose an heir from among the young ones. From what Mr. Entwistle knew, during the last six months of his life, Richard had invited them to live with him one by one: his nephew George, his niece Susan, and Susan's husband, his niece Rosamond and Rosamond's husband, and his sister-in-law, Helen. Mr. Entwistle estimated that the heir would be chosen from the first three. He also remembered that during those six months, Richard had briefly visited his brother Timothy.
[100] The final outcome was the will in Mr. Entwistle's briefcase, with all the assets evenly distributed. Therefore, the only conclusion was that he was disappointed in his nephews, nieces, and their spouses.
[101] Mr. Entwistle knew that Richard had not invited his sister, Cora Lanscourte to visit him - this made the lawyer remember Cora's incoherent outburst - "But I only heard what he said -"
[102] What did Richard Abernathy say? When did he say it? If Cora had not come to Endebi, then Richard Abernathy must have visited her at her small villa in the artistic village of Berkshire. Or perhaps Richard said something in a letter to her?
[103] Mr. Entwistle furrowed his brow. Of course, Cora was a very foolish woman. She would easily misunderstand a statement and distort its meaning. However, he was curious about what could have been misinterpreted as...
[104] This strong unease made him consider whether he should visit Mrs. Lanscourte. He shouldn't rush, and it would be best to act as if nothing was wrong. But he wanted to find out what Richard Abernathy had said to her, allowing her to casually blurt out that shocking statement:
[105] "But he was murdered, wasn't he?"

[107] In the third-class carriage of the same train, Greg Banks told his wife:
[108] "Your aunt is a lunatic!"
[109] "Cora Aunt?" Susan said vaguely. "Oh, I suppose she's a bit too simple-minded."
[110] George Crosfield sat across from them, his tone sharp:
[111]"We must stop her from saying such things, people will be thinking all sorts of things."
[112]Rosalind Shain held a lipstick and carefully outlined her Cupid's bow lips, muttering:
[113]"I don't think anyone will believe a word from a rumpled old hag like her, look at the odd clothes she wears and all those beads and trinkets –"
[114]"Still, I think we must stop her," George said.
[115] "Well, dear," Rosalind smiled, putting away her lipstick and admiring herself in the mirror, "You go and stop her."
[116] Her husband suddenly interjected:
[117] "I agree with George's point of view. It's easy to start rumors."
[118] "So what if it is?" Rosamond pondered the question, her Cupid's bow lips curving upwards in a smile. "It should be quite interesting."
[119] "Interesting?" Four voices asked in unison.
[120] "There's a murder in the house," Rosamond replied. "Isn't that thrilling?"
[121] The tense and somber young man, Greg Banks, realized that besides her attractive appearance, this cousin of Susan's bore a resemblance to her aunt Cora. Rosa's next words further confirmed his thoughts.
[122] "If he was murdered," Rosa said, "who do you think did it?"
[123] She looked around the carriage thoughtfully.
[124] "His death would be beneficial to everyone," she mused. "Michael and I are at the end of our rope. Michael has a great opportunity in the 'Sandbourne Show' if he can hold out until then. Now we have the money to produce our own play. In fact, there's a really exciting role in one of the plays—"
[125] No one was listening to Rosa's rambling as she basked in her excitement. Their attention was focused on their own impending changes.
[126] "It's a close call," George thought. "Now I can make up the money and no one will ever know... almost slipped up."
[127] Greg closed his eyes and leaned back in his chair, trying to avoid interference from others.
[128] Susan spoke in her characteristic shrill voice: "I really feel sorry for poor Uncle Richard. But he's so old, and with Mortimer dead, there's no hope for him. Living like a vegetable for year after year must have been terrible for him. It's good for him to pass away quietly like this."
[129] Her sharp, confident gaze softened when she saw her husband. She loved Greg and always felt that he didn't love her as much as she loved him—which only increased her passion. Greg was hers, and she would do anything for him, anything—
[130] 3
[131] The Endebury Estate. Mod had changed into her clothes and was preparing to go down for dinner - she had decided to stay overnight. She didn't know if she should suggest staying a few more days to help Helen tidy and clean the house. It must be all Richard's personal belongings... maybe letters... She guessed that all the important documents should have been taken by Mr. Entwistle. She had to hurry back to Timothy as soon as possible. He was always very unsettled without her around to take care of him, and she hoped he would be happier when he found out about the will. She knew Timothy believed that most of Richard's property should be his, after all, he was the only surviving member of the Abbernathy family, Richard's younger brother. Richard could have easily passed on the younger generation to him to take care of. Yes, she imagined Timothy would be very angry when he found out about the will... It would be bad for his digestion. And to be honest, when he was angry, he wasn't very rational, and there were even times when he lost his temper... She wondered if she should talk to Dr. Bartholomew about this... The sleeping pills - Timothy had taken too many lately - whenever she tried to help him keep track of the medicine bottles, he would get angry. But the pills could be dangerous - Dr. Bartholomew had said so - the person taking them could become drowsy and forget they had already taken some - and then take more. Anything could happen! There wasn't much left in the bottle now, and it should have been more left over with normal dosages... Timothy never took his medicine seriously, never listened to her... Sometimes he was really difficult to deal with.
[132] She sighed - her mood instantly brightened. The days ahead would be easier... like, the garden...
[133] 4
[134] In the green living room, Helen Abbernathy sat by the fireplace, waiting for Mod to come down for dinner.
[135] She looked around, reminiscing about the old times spent with Leo and others in this house. In the past, this house was filled with laughter, but a house like this needed many people - lively children, bustling servants, grand parties, and roaring fires in the winter. When only a grieving old man lived here, the house became sad...
[136] She wondered who would buy this house? Would it be turned into a hotel or club, or a hostel for young people? Such huge mansions are usually disposed of in this way, no one would buy them to live in. Perhaps it would be demolished and rebuilt from scratch. The thought made her sad, but she quickly suppressed the feeling. Nostalgia is not a good thing. This house, yes, it was full of joy in the past, with Richard and Leo, everything was so beautiful, but it was all in the past. She had her own real concerns… and now, with the income Richard left her, she could carry out all her plans on her little estate in Cyprus.
[137] She had been troubled by money – taxes – all those failed investments… thanks to Richard's money, cash, it's all over…
[138] Poor Richard. It was too merciful for him to die peacefully in his sleep… it was on the 22nd, so sudden – she guessed that was why Cora had that idea. Cora was so nasty! She had always been. Helen remembered meeting her abroad, not long after she married Pierre Lanscoigne. On that occasion, she behaved so stupidly, it was ridiculous. She tilted her head and made arbitrary judgments about the paintings, especially her husband's works, those comments must have made him uncomfortable. No man could tolerate a fool for a wife, and Cora was a fool! Oh, never mind, poor thing, she couldn't help it, and her husband wasn't much better to her.
[139] Helen's gaze lingered on a bouquet of wax flowers on the malachite table, lost in thought. When everyone was sitting here waiting to go to church, Cora sat at that stone table, enthusiastically reminiscing about the past, excited by every memory.
[140] "Perhaps," Helen thought, "she is just not as hypocritical as we are…"
[141] Cora had never been one to pay attention to rules and manners. Look at how she blurted out that sentence: "But he was murdered, wasn't he?"
[142] Every face around her was shocked, staring at her in amazement! The expressions on their faces were so varied...
[143] The scene suddenly appeared in Helen's mind, and she frowned... There was something wrong with that picture...
[144] Something...
[145] Someone...
[146] Is it a particular expression on someone's face? Or is it... something—she doesn't know how to describe it—something that shouldn't be there...
[147] She's unsure... She can't pinpoint it... But there was definitely something, somewhere—something was off.
[148] 5
[149] Meanwhile, at a self-service restaurant in Swindon, a woman with black jade beads and wearing a tailored mourning dress is sipping tea and eating circular croissants, looking forward to her future. There's no hint of sorrow on her face; she's absolutely delighted.
[150] A journey across the entire country is certainly exhausting, but returning from London to Litchurch St. Mary is much easier—and costs almost the same. Now, the cost doesn't matter. She might have had to travel with her family, perhaps even have to talk to them along the way. Too much trouble.
[151] Yes, it's better to take this route. These croissants are delicious. Attending a funeral always makes one exceptionally hungry. The soup at Enfield is quite tasty—as well as the cold egg custard.
[152] How self-important those people are—how pretentious! The way they looked at her—when she mentioned murder! They all gaped at her like that!
[153] Well, that's quite right. She nodded approvingly. Yes, quite right.
[154] She glanced at her watch. She had five minutes before her train was due to depart. She drained her cup of tea. It wasn't very good, and she made a face.
[155] Suddenly, she began to daydream about her future. She smiled like a happy child when she thought about it.
[156] Finally, she could indulge herself... She planned in her mind as she left the restaurant and made her way to a small train on a branch line...
[157] Chapter Four
[158] Mr. Entwistle tossed and turned all night. When he woke up in the morning, he still felt tired and uncomfortable, so he didn't get up.
[159] His sister, who took care of the household chores, brought breakfast to his bedside in a tray, scolding him for his age and poor health for traveling all the way to North England.
[160] Mr. Entwistle explained that Richard Abernathy was an old friend of his.
[161] "Funeral!" his sister's tone sounded even more disapproving, "A funeral! At your age, you should not be attending a funeral, it's dangerous! If you don't take better care of yourself, you'll end up like that precious Mr. Abernathy, dropping dead suddenly."
[162] The word "suddenly" made Mr. Entwistle shudder. It also made him fall silent and not argue with her further.
[163] He knew very well why he would shudder at the word "suddenly."
[164] Cora Lanscot! What she hinted at could not possibly be true, but regardless, he decided to find out why she said that. Yes, he should go to Litchfield St. Mary to see her. He could use an excuse that there were some documents related to the probate of the will that needed her signature, without letting her know that he was investigating her silly remark. He should visit her – and do it quickly.
[165] He finished his breakfast, propping himself up on his pillows and picking up a copy of The Times. He found The Times particularly interesting today.
[166] At 5:45 p.m. that evening, the telephone rang.
[167] He answered it. The voice on the other end was James Parrot, the second partner of Bollard-Entwistle & Co.
[168] "Listen, Entwistle," Mr. Parrot said, "I just got a call from a police officer in a place called Litchet St. Mary."
[169] "Litchet St. Mary?"
[170] "Yes, it's about –" Mr. Parrot paused, his tone sounding a bit uncomfortable, "the call was about Cora Lanscot. She's not one of Mr. Abernathy's heirs, is she?"
[171] "Yes, of course. I met her at the funeral yesterday."
[172] "Oh, she attended the funeral, did she?"
[173] "Yes. What happened?"
[174] "Well," Mr. Parrot's tone carried a touch of regret, "she… this is too unusual… she was murdered."
[175] Mr. Parrot uttered the last few words with a pronounced sneer. He felt that such language should never be associated with Bollard-Entwistle & Co.
[176] "Murdered?"
[177] "Yes—I'm afraid so. Uh, I mean, it's been confirmed."
[178] "How did the police get in touch with us?"
[179] "It was Miss Gilchrist, her maid or housekeeper, I suppose. The police asked her for the names of Miss Cola's next of kin and lawyers, and Miss Gilchrist seemed unfamiliar with her relatives and their addresses, but she knew us, so they contacted me right away."
[180] "What makes them think it was murder?" Mr. Entwistle pressed on.
[181] Mr. Parrot answered apologetically.
[182] "Well, as far as that goes, there's no doubt about it — I mean, the weapon seems to be an axe or something — a very violent way of killing."
[183] "A burglary?"
[184] "That's a possibility. The window was broken, some cheap trinkets were stolen, and the drawers were all gone through, but the police seem to think that — uh — it might have been faked."
[185] "When did it happen?"
[186] "Sometime between two o'clock and four-thirty this afternoon."
[187] "Where was the maid at the time?"
[188] "She went to the library in Reading to return a book. When she came back at five o'clock, she found Mrs. Lanscourt dead. The police wanted to know if we knew anyone who might have done it, and I told them, "Mr. Parrot spoke indignantly, "that it's the most unlikely thing in the world."
[189] "Yes, of course."
[190] "It was some local fool – wanted to steal something, got carried away, and killed her. That's the way it must have been – well, you agree, Entwistle?"
[191] "Yes, yes…" Mr. Entwistle answered absently.
[192] He told himself that Mr. Parrot was right. It must have been like that…
[193] But the words of Miss Cora, spoken with such glee, echoed in his ears:
[194] "But he was murdered, wasn't he?"
[195] Idiot, Cora, always an idiot. So reckless… saying such distasteful truths…
[196] Truths!
[197] That damned word again…
[198] 2
[199] Mr. Entwistle and Inspector Morton regarded each other.
[200] At the inspector's command, Mr. Entwistle meticulously gathered all the information related to Miss Cora Lanescott: her background, marriage, widowhood, financial situation, and relatives.
[201] "Timothy Abner West is her only surviving brother and her closest relative, but he is a recluse and physically weak, unable to leave his home. He has authorized me to make all necessary arrangements if needed."
[202] The inspector nodded. Dealing with the shrewd old lawyer was a relief. He relied on the lawyer to help him solve the perplexing puzzle at hand.
[203] He said:
[204] "I learned from Miss Gilchrist that Mrs. Lanscot attended her brother's funeral in the north the day before she was murdered?"
[205] "Indeed, Inspector. I was present at the time."
[206] "Was there anything unusual, strange, or worrying about her behavior during that time?"
[207] Mr. Entwistle looked surprised and raised his eyebrows.
[208] "Does a person about to be murdered usually behave abnormally?"
[209] The inspector chuckled.
[210] "I don't mean that she showed any signs of excitement or any unusual behavior before her death. It's just that I wanted to find some details – ah, some different details from usual."
[211] "I'm not quite sure what you mean, Inspector."
[212] "This case is quite hard to understand, Mr. Entwistle. The killer watched Miss Gilchrist as she left the house around 2 pm, walking to the village and the bus stop. The killer took an axe from the shed, smashed the glass, entered the house, went upstairs, and brutally murdered Mrs. Lanscourt - she was hacked to death, around six to eight times. Then, the killer rummaged through some drawers, taking some worthless trinkets - worth at most ten pounds - and left."
[213] "Was she in bed?"
[214] "Yes. She had arrived very late from the north, very tired but very excited. I understand she inherited some money?"
[215] "Yes."
[216] "She didn't sleep well and woke up with a headache. She had some tea and took some painkillers and told Miss Gilchrist not to disturb her before lunch. She still felt unwell, so she took two more sleeping pills, and then she told Miss Gilchrist to take the bus to the library in Reading to exchange some books for her. The killer entered the room when she was probably drowsy, even if she wasn't asleep. He could have threatened her and taken whatever he wanted, or easily stuffed her mouth. It seems a bit excessive to bring a hatchet from the outside."
[217] "He might have just wanted to use the hatchet to scare her," speculated Mr. Entwistle, "if she resisted -"

[219] Mr. Entwistle shifted uneasily in his seat.
[220] "I have heard of such cruel and senseless murders before," he said.
[221] "Yes, that's likely what this is," the inspector agreed. "We've checked everyone who had a motive. None of the locals are suspected, and we're quite sure of that. We've ruled them all out. Most of them were at work at the time. Of course, her cottage is at the end of a narrow lane outside the village, and anyone could have approached it unnoticed. The alleys around the village are like a maze. There were no tire tracks from cars that morning—assuming someone drove past."
[222] "Do you think the killer drove there?" Mr. Entwistle asked suddenly.

[230] "I didn't say that."

[233] "That's right. Her husband was in dire straits when he died, and I've known her since she was a little girl. It would be strange if she had ever saved a penny."
[234] "The cottage was rented, not hers, and those few pieces of furniture, even now, are hardly worth mentioning - some cheap imitation oak furniture and some pretentious paintings."
[235] Mr. Entwistle shook his head.

[238] "Yes, I do have some ideas. I hope I can express myself more accurately."

[240] "No, not as you might think. She doesn't have the power to dispose of the principal. Now that she's dead, the inheritance will be divided equally among the other five heirs of Richard Abner-West. That's what I mean. The moment she dies, the other five beneficiaries automatically benefit."
[241] The inspector looked disappointed.
[242] "Ah, I thought there was a clue. Well, it seems that no one has a motive to come and chop her down with an axe. It looks like it was done by some crazy guy – maybe some juvenile delinquents – there are plenty of them, they kill someone, get scared, and then throw the jewelry into the bushes and run away... Yes, that's probably it. Unless it was Miss Gilchrist – I must say, that's almost impossible."
[243] "When did she discover the body?"
[244] "Around five o'clock. She took the 4:40 bus back from the library in Reading, arrived at the cottage, entered through the front door, and boiled some water to make tea. There was no movement in Mrs. Lanscourt's room, and Miss Gilchrist assumed she might still be sleeping. Then she noticed the kitchen window, with broken glass all over the floor. Even at that point, she thought it might have been a kid who broke it with a ball or a slingshot. She quietly went upstairs, into Mrs. Lanscourt's room, to see if she was still sleeping or had woken up to have some tea. Then, as you can imagine, she screamed in terror and rushed to the nearest neighbor's house. Her story seems to match the facts, and there's no blood on her room, bathroom, or clothes. No, I don't think Miss Gilchrist has anything to do with this case. The doctor arrived at the scene at 5:30 and determined that the time of death was no later than 4:00 – likely around 2:00. It seems that whoever the killer was, they were waiting nearby for Miss Gilchrist to leave."

[246] "Yes, I do."

[327] Mr. Entwistle found this statement equally irritating, although his thoughts were not quite the same as Miss Gilchrist's.
[328] Was Cora Lanscott really clear? Perhaps she didn't realize it at the time, but realized it later. Or did she overthink it?
[329] Mr. Entwistle knew that there were no signs of organ failure in Richard Abernathy. His bodily functions were always good, and he would never have any form of persecutory delusions. He was, and always had been, a cool-headed businessman – disease had not affected this trait of his.
[330] It was unusual for him to tell his sister such things. Perhaps it was Miss Gilchrist herself – her ideas were always fanciful, like a child – who had taken each word from her brother and made sense of it.

[338] "I believe that can be arranged," Mr. Entwistle replied kindly.

[340] Chapter Five
[341] "Look at yourself, all worn out," Miss Entwistle said with the stern tone of an older sister to a younger brother, "At your age, you shouldn't be doing such things. I'm curious, what does this have to do with you? You've retired, haven't you?"

[548] "That's it," she said. "This old guy has been playing tricks on me lately. The last time I came home from the funeral, it was like this, and I had to walk several miles to find a garage. I had to call him and tell him I wouldn't be back until the next day. He was worried sick. I always try to keep things from him – but some things can't be helped – like Cora being murdered. I had to ask Mr. Bardon to give Timothy a sedative. With Timothy's health, murder is too much to bear. I think Cora was just a fool.
[549] Mr. Entwistle digested the last sentence silently, not knowing what she was referring to.
[550] "I haven't seen Cora since I got married," Mrs. Abernethy said. "I couldn't bear to tell Timothy, 'Your sister is crazy.' Of course, I didn't think that at the time, but that's how I felt. She used to say the strangest things, making it hard to tell whether to be angry or laugh. I guess she lived in her own world, filled with dramas and fanciful notions about other people. Poor thing, she got what she deserved. She didn't have any friends, did she?"
[551] "Friends? You mean?"
[552] "I'm just guessing. Some freeloading young artist or musician – that sort of person. Maybe they were staying with her and killed her for some cash. Maybe it was a teenager – that age can be hard to figure out – especially when they're pretentious and neurotic. I mean, breaking into a house in broad daylight to kill someone is just bizarre. If you're going to break in, you'd do it at night."
[553] "Then there would be two people in the house, not just Cora."
[554] "Oh, yes, the maid. I can't believe someone would wait for her to leave and then break in to attack Cora. For what? He couldn't think she had any money or anything worth stealing, and if it was for money, they could have waited until both of them were gone, which would have been safer. Unless they were forced to, murder is just stupid."
[555] "So, you think Cora's murder was a random act?"
[556] "I think it's too dumb."
[557] Does a murder have to make sense? Entwistle thought. Theoretically, yes. But there are plenty of senseless murders. He thought, it depends on the state of the killer's mind.
[558] What does he know about the killer and their mental state? Not much. His company has never handled a murder case, and he has no background in criminology. According to his judgment, there are all kinds of killers. Some are driven by excessive vanity, some crave power. Some, like Siddon, are despicably greedy; others, like Smith and Ross, are overly obsessed with women; some, like Armstrong, are friendly when interacting with others. Edith Thompson lives in a world of violence, and Waddington the nurse happily kills the old patients, treating it like a routine task…
[559] Maud's voice interrupted his thoughts.
[560] "If I had just hidden the newspaper so Timmy couldn't see it! But he insisted on looking - and of course, the news made him very upset. You can understand, can't you, Mr. Entwistle? Timmy can't possibly attend the inquest."
[561] "You needn't worry about that."
[562] "Thank goodness!"
[563] The car turned into the drive of Stanfield Hall, driving on a dilapidated lane. This small estate used to be charming—now it's in disrepair due to lack of maintenance. Maud sighed and said:
[564] "We had to let it go during the war. Both gardeners were conscripted, and now there's only an old man—who's not very good, but his wages have skyrocketed. I must say, I'm so grateful that we can finally afford to renovate this place. My husband and I both love it here. I was really worried that we might have to sell it… I never told Timmy. If he found out, he'd be devastated."
[565] The car stopped in front of the porch. It was a very old Georgian-style building that needed a fresh coat of paint.
[566] "No servants," Maud said with a hint of bitterness as she led Enthwistle inside, "only a few women who come to help. We had a full-time maid just a month ago—slightly hunched, with a very swollen gland and not very bright, but she was a great help in the kitchen. Can you believe she quit to work for another woman who had six Pekingese dogs—the house must have been bigger, and there was more work—she said she 'loved little dogs'? Dogs! I can't think of anything they're good for except getting sick and causing trouble. Honestly, the girls who keep dogs are all a bit neurotic! So now, if I have to go out on an afternoon and leave Timothy at home alone, what would he do if something happened? I've put the phone right next to his chair. He can call Dr. Bardon if he doesn't feel well."
[567] Maud led Enthwistle into the living room, where tea was already prepared and placed by the fireplace. After inviting him to sit down, she disappeared into another room. The tea was excellent, accompanied by homemade cake and fresh rolls. Enthwistle asked softly:

[569] Maud cheerfully explained that she had already served him tea and snacks on a tray before she left for the station.

[571] Mr. Entwistle assured her that he would be very careful.
[572] He looked at her in the flickering firelight, feeling a touch of sympathy. Maud Abernathy was a natural mother without children. She treated her ailing husband as a child who needed her care and protection. Perhaps, as the stronger partner in the relationship, her nature inadvertently made her husband more feeble.
[573] "Poor Timothy's wife," Mr. Entwistle thought to himself.

[597] Timothy kicked off the blanket and sat upright in the chair, his emaciation and weakness swept away. Mr. Entwistle thought he looked very healthy, even excited. The old lawyer realized that Timothy Abbernathy had always secretly envied his brother Richard. They looked alike, and Timothy had always resented his strong-willed brother's hold on the family. When Richard died, Timothy eagerly anticipated inheriting the power over his life. But Richard had not given him that power. Had he ever considered it, but changed his mind?

I can give you a brief summary of my conclusions. George could have been betting on horses at Hastings Racecourse, but I don't think he was telling the truth. [721] Mr. Entwistle took some time to recount his visit to Timothy and Maud. Poirot summarized the key points.
[722] "So, Mrs. Abney-Waller is quite knowledgeable about machinery. She knows the workings of the car in its entirety, and Mr. Abney-Waller is not as weak as he thinks he is. He can go for walks and, based on your description, do quite strenuous activities. Meanwhile, he is somewhat arrogant and envious of his brother's success and self-importance."
[723] "But he spoke very sentimentally about Cora."
[724] "Yet he mocked her for the foolish things she said at the funeral. What about the sixth beneficiary?"
[725] "Helen? Mrs. Leigh? I have no doubt about her. And anyway, her innocence is easily proven. She was in England, with three servants, at her estate."
[726] "Well, my friend," said Poirot, "let's be practical. What do you want me to do?"
[727] "I want to find out the truth, Poirot."
[728] "Yes, yes. If I were you, I would have the same idea."
[729] "And you are the one who can help me find the truth. I know you no longer take cases, but I would like to engage your services. This is a professional matter, and I will cover your fees. So, agree to it, and make some extra money. It won't do any harm."
[730] Poirot grinned.
[731] "The more you have, the more you pay in taxes! But I agree, this case interests me. It's difficult... shrouded in mystery... and there's one thing, my friend, that you need to do. After that, I will take care of everything. I think it would be best if you went to see the doctor who took care of Richard Abernathy. Do you know him?"
[732] "I know him."
[733] "What kind of person is he?"
[734] "He's a middle-aged general practitioner. Very competent. He and Richard were good friends, and he's a thoughtful person."
[735] "Then please go see him. He should be more relaxed talking to you than to me. [736] Mr. Entwistle took a moment to recall.
[737] "Yes, it was that word - but she's a witness who could change her testimony at any time because she's so confident in her own associations. I'll make time to talk to her again about this."
[738] "Yes, or I can go instead." Mr. Poirot paused and changed his tone. "My friend, have you ever thought that Miss Gilchrist might be in danger?"
[739] Mr. Entwistle looked surprised.
[740] "I never thought of that."
[741] "But she is in danger. At the funeral, Cora voiced her suspicions. The killer might have wondered if she had told anyone about her suspicions after Richard's death. [744] "She wants to see what Cora left behind."

[746] Poirot was brimming with energy, stroking his mustache.
[747] Chapter Eight
[748] Mr. Entwistle watched Dr. Lorabie with a heavy heart.
[749] He had seen many people in his life, had a lot of experience, and often encountered difficult situations or delicate topics that were hard to handle. Mr. Entwistle was very adept at handling such matters. The question was how to approach Dr. Lorabie, as he might become angry if he thought his medical skills were being questioned.
[750] Honesty - Mr. Entwistle thought - at least slightly modified honesty. Tell him that because of an unintentional remark by a silly woman, there are suspicions about Richard's cause of death, which would surely affect his reputation as a doctor. Dr. Lorabie doesn't know Cora.
[751] Mr. Entwistle cleared his throat and mustered the courage to speak.
[752] "I would like to ask you a very delicate matter," he said, "that may offend you, but I sincerely hope it won't. You are a reasonable person, and I believe you will understand that the best way to handle such an absurd suggestion is to give a reasonable answer rather than to reprimand. This matter concerns my client – Richard Abernathy. I would like to ask you this question directly. Are you absolutely certain, completely certain, that he died of natural causes?"
[753] Dr. Larrabee's normally rosy, pleasant face was suddenly filled with surprise. He looked at the man who had asked the question.
[754] "You mean – he certainly died of natural causes. I signed the certificate, didn't I? If I wasn't sure –"
[755] Mr. Entwistle deftly interrupted him.
[756] "Of course, of course. I assure you that I have no personal suspicions. But I would like to hear your positive assurance – given – uh – given the current rumors."
[757] "Rumors? What rumors?"
[758] "I don't know where it came from," Mr. Entwistle lied, "but I believe that such rumors should be stopped – if possible, by you."
[759] "Abernathy was a patient. The disease he had was already proven to be fatal within two years, I dare say. It could be even earlier. The death of his son reduced his will to live and his strength to fight the disease. I admit, I didn't expect him to die so soon or so suddenly, but such cases have happened – many times. Any doctor who claims to be able to accurately predict when a patient will die or how long they can still live is fooling themselves. Human factors are unpredictable. The weak sometimes have unexpected resistance, while the strong can be easily overwhelmed by illness."
[760] "I understand all that. I am not questioning your diagnosis. Mr. Abernathy was – let's say, perhaps too dramatically – sentenced to death, and I would like to ask you if it is possible for a person who knows or anticipates that their time is limited to shorten their own life or for someone else to do it for them."
[761] Dr. Larrabee furrowed his brow.
[762] "You mean suicide? Mr. Abner West is not a suicidal person."
[763] "I understand. From a medical perspective, you can assure me that such an assumption is impossible."
[764] The doctor shifted uneasily.
[765] "I wouldn't say impossible. For Mr. Abner West, life has lost much of its meaning since the death of his son. I certainly don't think there's a possibility of suicide - but I can't say it's absolutely impossible."
[766] "Your conclusion is based on a psychological analysis, isn't it? What I meant by 'medical perspective' was: in terms of his circumstances at the time of his death, is that so?"
[767] "No, no, no. No, I wouldn't say that. He passed away peacefully in his sleep. There's no reason to suspect suicide, and there are no signs in his mindset. If every critically ill person who passed away in their sleep required an autopsy—"
[768] The doctor's face grew increasingly red. Mr. Entwistle hastily reined in his words.
[769] "Of course, of course. But what if there were signs—something you might have missed. For example, if he had mentioned—"
[770] "Did he hint at planning to commit suicide? Did he say anything? I must say, I'm quite surprised."
[771] "But if that were the case—just a hypothetical assumption—could you rule out that possibility?"
[772] Dr. Lorbeau spoke slowly. "No—no—I can't. But I'll reiterate that if that were the case, I would be very surprised."
[773] Mr. Entwistle pursued the point. "So, if we assume he didn't die of natural causes—this is just a pure hypothesis—what caused his death? I mean, which drug?"
[774] "There were a few possibilities, possibly some anesthetic. His skin didn't turn purple, and his demeanor was calm at the time of death."
[775] "Did he take any sleeping pills or sleeping medication, or something similar?"
[776] "Yes. I have prescribed sleeping pills to him - they are safe and reliable sleep aids. He doesn't take them every night, and I only give him a small bottle at a time. Based on the dosage I gave him, consuming three to four times the amount would not be fatal. In fact, after his death, I noticed that the pill bottle on his vanity was almost full."
[777] "Have you prescribed him any other drugs?"
[778] "There are many kinds – there's a drug with a small amount of morphine that he can take when he's in pain. There are also vitamin capsules and digestive aids."
[779] Mr. Entwistle interrupted him.
[780] "Vitamin capsules? I think I've taken them before, small round capsules."
[781] "Yes, there were also vitamin AD."
[782] "Could anything else have been mixed in - uh - one of the capsules?"
[783] "Are you suggesting something deadly?" The doctor's expression becomes increasingly incredulous. "But surely no one - listen, Mr. Entwistle, are you implying murder?"
[784] "I'm not quite sure what I'm implying... I just want to know if there's a possibility."
[785] "But what evidence do you have to suggest such a thing?"
[786] "I have no evidence," Mr. Enthwistle's voice was weary, "Mr. Abnerc died—those who heard him mention it are also dead. I assure you, it would lighten my burden considerably."
[787] Dr. Laroche stood up and began pacing back and forth.
[788] "I can't tell you what you want me to say," he finally said, "I wish I could, of course it's possible. [884] Chapter 9
[885] Miss Gilchrist carefully placed her black hat on her head, tucking a stray grey hair back inside. The inquest was set for noon, and it was only a few minutes before eleven twenty. She thought the gray coat and skirt looked nice. She had also bought a black blouse for herself. She had hoped to wear all black, but it was beyond her means. She looked around the neat little bedroom, where the walls were adorned with sketches – of Brickleham Harbour, the Blacksmith's Forge at Cokington, the Ansty River Bend, the Kyne's River Bend, Belfry's Landing, and Babbacombe Bay. All the paintings bore the signature of Cora Lanscot, Esq. Her gaze lingered on her favorite one, Belfry's Landing. A framed photograph hung on the wardrobe, slightly faded, of Willow House. Miss Gilchrist gazed at it with fondness, sighing.
[886] The doorbell downstairs suddenly rang, jolting her out of her reverie.

[938] There seemed to be many strangers present at the inquest. [1153] Chapter 11
[1154] Susan lay in bed, waiting for sleep to come. After such a long day, she felt really tired. She thought she would fall asleep quickly, so she lay in bed, hour after hour, still unable to sleep, with her eyes wide open and her mind racing.
[1155] She had said she didn't mind sleeping in this room, on this bed. On this very bed, Cora Lanscotte had -
[1156] No, no, she had to push those thoughts out of her mind. She prided herself on her calmness. Why think about what happened just a few days ago, on that afternoon? Think about the future – the future. Hers and Greg's future. The property on Cardigan Street – exactly what they wanted. Downstairs for business, upstairs for a cozy home, and a room at the back for Greg's lab. This way, they could save a lot on taxes. Greg would become calm and normal again, no longer occasionally daydreaming. He would look at her with a puzzled expression, as if he didn't know who she was. There were times when she really got scared… and Mr. Coral – he had hinted – threatened to say, "If there's another one…" There really could be another one – there would be another time. If Uncle Richard hadn't died at that moment…
[1157] Uncle Richard – but why did she have to think about that? He had no reason to continue living. He was old, tired, and sick. His son was dead. That would have been a relief for him. A peaceful death in his sleep. Peaceful… in his sleep… If only she could sleep. She had no reason to lie here hour after hour, unable to sleep… listening to the creaking of the furniture, the rustling of the branches and shrubs outside the window, and occasionally a mournful hoot – she guessed it was an owl. The countryside had a strange, eerie feeling, different from the noisy, indifferent town. It felt safer to live in the town – surrounded by people – not feeling alone, and here…
[1158] Houses where murders had occurred sometimes have ghosts, and this villa might become famous as a haunted house. The ghost of Cora Lanscronnet is wandering around… Cora Aunt. It's strange, really, since she arrived here, she felt Cora Aunt was very close… at her fingertips. It was all just fancy and overactive imagination. Cora Lanscronnet was dead, and she would be buried tomorrow. There was no one else in the house except Susan and Miss Gilchrist. But why did she always feel like there was someone else in the house, hiding beside her…
[1159] When the axe was swung at her, she lay on this bed… sleeping defenselessly… and now she doesn't let Susan sleep…
[1160] The sound of the furniture creaking again… was it the sound of sneaky footsteps? Susan turned on the light. Nothing, just overactive imagination, nothing but overactive imagination… close your eyes…
[1161] That was indeed a moan—a moan or a faint sigh…someone was suffering in agony…
[1162] "I must not think of these things anymore, I must not, I cannot." Susan whispered to herself.
[1163] Death is the end—there will be nothing after that. The dead cannot return. Is she reliving that moment—a dying woman moaning in pain…
[1164] The sound again…louder…someone was moaning in agony…
[1165] But—it's a real sound. Susan turns on the light again and sits up to listen carefully. The moaning is real, and she can hear it through the walls. It's coming from the next room.
[1166] Susan jumps out of bed, hastily puts on her robe, and goes out of the room. She walks to the staircase, gently knocks on Miss Gilchrist's door, and goes in. The light is on, Miss Gilchrist is sitting on the bedside, her face pale and ghastly, her expression twisted with pain.

[1168] "Yes. I don't know how – I –" She struggles to get up, suddenly vomiting violently and collapsing on the pillow.

[1170] "I'll get some soda water for you. If you're not better in the morning, we'll find a doctor."
[1171] Miss Gilchrist shook her head.
[1172] "No, call now. I—I feel terrible."

[1174] Miss Gilchrist told her the phone number, only to vomit again.

[1177] As he said, ten minutes later, Susan heard the sound of a car pulling up outside, and she opened the door.

[1312] Chapter Twelve
[1313] The room was strikingly modern, with two elderly people sitting together. There were no curves in the room, everything was square. The only exception was Hercule Poirot himself, who was full of curves. His belly was a pleasing circle, his head resembled an egg, and his mustache proudly curved upwards.
[1314] He took a sip of syrup, his mind troubled as he looked at Mr. Cubby.
[1315] Mr. Cubby was small in stature, as if he had shrunk. He had a clean, unremarkable appearance, and now he seemed so inconspicuous that it was as if he wasn't even in the room. He didn't turn to look at Poirot, for Mr. Cubby never looked at anyone.
[1316] The words he spoke seemed to be directed at the corner of the chrome fireplace grate to his left.
[1317] Mr. Cubby had a knack for gathering information. Few knew of him, and even fewer hired him—but those who did were all extremely wealthy. It was only natural, as Mr. Cubby's services were quite expensive. His specialty was in quickly gathering information. Under his command were hundreds of diligent, patient men and women of all ages and backgrounds, at the ready to carry out his inquiries, investigations, and retrieval of results.
[1318] Mr. Cubby had retired, but occasionally accepted a few commissions from his old clients. Hercule Poirot was one of them.
[1319] "I have done my best to gather the information for you," Mr. Cubby murmured to the fireplace grate, "I have sent out the lads. They have done their best—all good men, but these days they are not what they used to be. They don't want to learn, that's the trouble. They do a year or two, think they know it all, and won't work overtime, not for a minute more. It's quite unbelievable."
[1320] He shook his head sadly and turned his gaze to a power outlet.
[1321] "It's all the government's fault," he said to the outlet. "And the nonsense of education. They have their own ideas. They come back and tell me what they think. Most of them don't think at all. They just know what's in the books, and that's no use to us in our line of work. Find the answer – that's all that matters – you don't have to think."
[1322] Mr. Goby leaned back in his chair and winked at a lampshade.
[1323] "It's not all the government's fault! We wouldn't know what to do without them. Tell you, you can almost get into any place now with a pencil, a notebook, dressed well, speaking standard British Broadcasting Corporation English, and asking people the most intimate details of their daily lives and their background experiences, or what they ate on November twenty-third, because that's a test day for middle-class earners – or any excuse, flattering them and buttering them up! You can ask them anything. They'll answer nine times out of ten – and if they don't, they won't even suspect your identity – the government usually asks such puzzling questions! I can tell you, Mr. Poirot, that this is a wonderful opportunity we've never had before, better than when we used to pretend to read the meters or fix the phone – or pretend to be nuns or Boy Scouts or Girl Guides to collect donations – although we still use those methods. Yes, the government's meddling is a gift from God for us investigators, and I hope it lasts forever!"
[1324] Poirot remained silent. Mr. Goby became more and more verbose as he grew older, but he would always get back to the point.
[1325] "Ah," Mr. Goby said, taking out a crumpled little notebook and licking his finger, flipping through it, "here is George Crosfield. Let's start with him. I'll just stick to the facts, and you won't care how I got them. He's been in trouble for some time. Mostly gambling on horses or at the tables – not very successful with women. He often goes to France, plays Monte Carlo, spends most of his time in the casinos. He's very clever, doesn't cash his checks locally, but he usually carries far more cash than his travel allowance. I didn't investigate that too closely, as it's not what you're interested in. But he's not afraid to exploit legal loopholes – as a lawyer, he knows how to do it. There's reason to believe he's embezzled client trust funds. He's been losing badly lately – both in the stock market and at the tables! Poor judgment and bad luck. He's been eating poorly for the past three months. He's always worried and irritable when he's at the office, and he's quick to anger. He's changed completely since his uncle's death. He's like a fried egg – according to our habits – turn him over and he's sunny side up!"
[1326] "Now, let's talk about the specific information you want. His talk about betting on the horse race at Hastings was all lies. He was placing bets through one or two fixed intermediaries, and they didn't see him that day. He might have left Paddington by train, with an unknown destination. A taxi driver who picked up passengers at Paddington looked at his photo and said it might be him. However, I'm not counting on that. His appearance is quite ordinary – nothing remarkable – nothing special. I also asked around at Paddington porters and others, but found nothing. It's certain that he didn't get off at Jowater – that's the closest station to Litchfield St. Mary. It's a small station, and strangers are always conspicuous. He might have gotten off at Reading and taken a bus from there. There are many buses from Reading that can get you within a mile of Litchfield St. Mary, and there's even a bus that goes directly to the village. He wouldn't have taken the direct bus – if he had any plans at all. Overall, he's a crafty guy. No one in Litchfield St. Mary saw him, but he could easily slip under the radar. Through other means, not directly from the village. By the way, he was part of the Oxford Dramatic Society. If he had gone to the cottage that day, he would have been dressed differently from his usual George Croxford attire. I'll keep looking into this, okay? I'm planning to start with his black market activities."
[1327] "We can continue to investigate," said Hercule Poirot.
[1328] Mr. Goby licked his fingers and turned the notebook to another page.
[1329] "Michael Shan. He's ambitious and expects more from himself than people expect. He wants to be famous overnight and be known by everyone. He likes money and spends it lavishly. He's very attractive to women, and they always follow him around. He enjoys it himself - but his career is still first and foremost. He hooked up with the leading lady of a play he was in, Sorel Danton. He was only a minor character at the time, but he made a big impression. Mr. Danton didn't like him. Michael's wife doesn't know about him and Sorel, and it seems she doesn't know much. [1332] "Of course."

[1338] "As I mentioned earlier, we have not yet determined Mr. Banks' whereabouts on the day of the murder of Lady Lanscourt. After all, such accidental errors are difficult to avoid, and most forgiving people would forgive this young man's momentary lapse—in other words, as long as no irreversible harm was done."
[1339] "Possible," said Hercule Poirot.
[1340] "In any case, he was admitted to a sanatorium for treatment and was discharged after recovery. He then met Miss Abney.
