[1913] Chapter XVIII
[1914] Hercule Poirot sat by the fireplace in the library, watching the people gathered around him.
[1915] His gaze swept over Susan, who sat upright, looking alert and full of energy; over her husband, who sat beside her, his expression blank, fiddling with a coil; then to George Croftsfield, who seemed in good spirits and self-satisfied, chatting with Rosamond about the card cheats they had encountered on their Atlantic cruise, Rosamond responding mechanically, "How unusual, but, dear, why?" her voice devoid of interest; then to the handsome Michael, who exuded a wild, attractive charm; to Helen, who was composed and somewhat distant; Poirot then looked at Timothy, who lounged comfortably in the best armchair, with an extra cushion behind him; and to the stout, sturdy Moira, who attended to her husband with concentration; and finally to Miss Gilcrist, who sat at the edge of the circle, with an apologetic expression, dressed in an overly "smart" blouse. He judged that it would not be long before she rose, found an excuse to leave the family gathering, and returned to her room. Miss Gilcrist, he thought, had learned her lesson the hard way.
[1916] Hercule Poirot took a sip of his after-dinner coffee, half-closing his eyes, and began to ponder.
[1917] He wanted them all to come – together – and they had come. What next, he thought, now what should he do with them? Suddenly, a sense of weariness overcame him, and he lost interest in pursuing the matter further. Why was he feeling this way, he wondered? Was it due to Miss Helen Abberline's influence? She seemed to have a trait of passive resistance, and this trait was surprisingly strong. She appeared indifferent and composed, but had she not imprinted this unwillingness onto his mind? He knew she was against investigating the family after Richard's death. She wanted to hush things up and let people gradually forget. Poirot was not surprised by this, what surprised him was that he had begun to lean towards her.
[1918] He realized that Mr. Entwistle's evaluation of the family was very accurate. His descriptions of each person were spot-on. Under the guidance of the old lawyer's understanding and evaluation of the family, Poirot wanted to observe them with his own eyes. He had imagined that as soon as he saw them, he would come up with a brilliant idea, not about "means" and "time" – these two issues he did not intend to delve into, but only about "who". Because Hercule Poirot had a lifetime of detective experience and could identify the author just by looking at a painting, he believed that as soon as he saw the amateur murderer, the criminal who was ready to kill at any moment, he would immediately recognize him.
[1919] But things would not be as simple as he had imagined.
[1920] Almost everyone present, he could imagine the possibility of becoming a murderer – although this possibility was not necessarily large for all of them. George could have killed – desperate and cornered. Susan was calm, capable, and could meticulously plan. Greg was peculiar and sickly, suspicious and eager or even eager to punish. Michael had ambition and the self-conceited vanity that a killer often possessed. As for Rosemary, her perspective on things was frighteningly simple. Timothy was motivated by his resentment towards his brother and his desire for the power that came with his brother's wealth. Mod would do anything for her child, and she could become cold-blooded and ruthless for that. Even Miss Gilchester, he thought, could have committed murder if she had the chance to revive the "Willow House" and regain her former glory as a lady. As for Miss Helen, he did not think she would kill. She was too noble – too far from violence. And it was certain that she and her husband loved Richard Abberline very much.
[1921] Poirot sighed inwardly. There was no shortcut to the truth. Instead, he planned to adopt a more time-consuming, but more reasonable and prudent method. He would have to talk, a lot. Because over time, whether through lies or through the truth, people would inevitably give themselves away…
[1922] He had been introduced by Helen and had done his best to overcome the resistance caused by his presence – a foreigner appearing unexpectedly at a family gathering. He had fully utilized his visual and auditory senses. Whether openly or secretly – he observed, listened, and paid close attention to the offhand remarks that were made, whether intimate, distant, or absent during the distribution of the inheritance. He skillfully arranged for them to have private conversations with him, accompanied them on walks around the estate, and then drew conclusions and inferences. He had talked with Miss Gilchrist about the former glory of her tea shop, the correct recipes for creamed eggs and chocolate éclairs, and they had even discussed the use of herbs in cooking while visiting the garden. He spent several long hours listening to Timothy discuss his health and the effects of paint on his body.
[1923] Paint? Poirot frowned. Who else had mentioned paint – Mr. Entwistle?
[1924] He had also discussed various paintings and the identity of Pierre Lanscotte, as well as Cora's works – which Miss Gilchrist admired and Susan dismissed with a wave of her hand. "Like a postcard," she said. "She must have painted it from a postcard."
[1925] Miss Gilchrist was incensed by this comment and retorted sharply that her dear Lanscotte lady always painted from life.
[1926]"I'm sure she's lying," Miss Gilchester left the room, and Susan told Poirot. "In fact, I know she's lying. I just don't say it to spare the old lady's feelings."
[1927]"What do you know?"
[1928]Poirot observed Susan's firm, confident chin.
[1929]"This girl always so sure," he thought. "Perhaps one day, she will be too sure…"
[1930]Susan continued:
[1931] "I can tell you, but you must not tell Miss Gilchrist. There is a painting of Bervflechter Harbour, the harbour, the lighthouse, and the quay – all the amateur painters sit down to sketch from this angle. But the quay was destroyed in the war, and since Miss Cora's sketch was painted years ago, she could not have been sketching from the actual scene, could she? But the postcards still show the quay. There is one in her bedroom drawer. I imagine that Miss Cora completed her initial 'sketch' in Bervflechter Harbour and then, at home, she completed the painting by secretly comparing it to the postcard! How ridiculous, isn't it, how easily people can be unmasked."
[1932] "Yes, very ridiculous, as you say." He paused for a moment, thinking that it was a good opening line.
[1933] "You don't remember me, madam," he said, "but I remember you. It is not the first time I have seen you."
[1934] She looked at him, and Poirot nodded with interest.

[2018] Chapter 19
[2019] The entire family treated the representative of "U.N.A.R.C.O.", Monsieur Pontalier, with great respect. His choice to use an acronym for his cover was a wise one. Everyone took him at his word - even pretending to know all about "U.N.A.R.C.O."! How reluctant humans are to admit their ignorance! Rosamond was the only exception, who asked with a touch of suspicion, "What is that? I've never heard of it?" Fortunately, no one else was around to hear Polo's explanation of the organization, which he presented as if it were a world-renowned institution that any self-respecting person should know about, of course excluding Rosamond. She merely murmured, "Oh! More refugees. I'm sick of these refugees."
[2020] Thus, Monsieur Pontalier was deemed - an annoying but insignificant person. He seemed like a foreign trinket, an oddity that everyone tolerated but didn't really care about. It was generally accepted that Helen shouldn't have invited him for this particular weekend, but since he was already there, everyone had to make do. Luckily, this strange little foreigner seemed to not understand English very well. He often got confused by what people were saying, especially when several people spoke at once. He seemed only interested in refugees and post-war situations, and his vocabulary seemed to cover only those two topics. General chit-chat usually left him bewildered. In this somewhat forgotten situation, Hercule Poirot leaned back in his chair, sipping his coffee, and observed silently. Like a cat observing a group of chirping birds, this cat was not yet ready to pounce.
[2021] After spending twenty-four hours wandering around the house and examining various items, Richard Abernathy's heirs were ready to declare what they wanted and, if necessary, fight for it.

[2199] That night, Poirot slept poorly. He was restless and agitated, but he didn't know why. You're ready to admit that you overheard Richard's conversation with his sister. In fact, what he told her, without a doubt, was that he was not long for this world, which also explains the vague phrase in the letter he wrote to her after returning home. 'The nun' was another hint, the one — or rather, the two nuns who went to the small villa on the day of the inquest, inspiring you to mention 'a nun following you.' And when you were eager to eavesdrop on Timothy's conversation with her sister-in-law in Enfield, you used the same pretext. Poisoning yourself with arsenic, a serious but not fatal method, is a very traditional approach — and I must say, it was precisely this that led Inspector Morton to suspect you."
[2792] "What about the painting?" said Rosamond. "What kind of painting is it?"
[2793] Poirot slowly unfolded the telegram.
[2794] "This morning I called Mr. Entwistle, a very conscientious man, and asked him to go to Stanfield House, pretending to be on behalf of Mr. Abernethie, and," Polly glared at Timothy, "go to Miss Gilchrist's room, find a painting of Belfrey Harbour among the many there, and take it to London to be re-framed as a surprise for Miss Gilchrist. He took the painting to Mr. Guthrie, whom I had telegraphed, and when the preliminary sketch was removed, the original underneath was revealed."
[2795] He picked up the telegram and read it out loud.
[2796] "It is indeed a Vermeer. Gresham."
[2797] Suddenly, Miss Gilchrist gasped and burst out with a torrent of words.
[2798] "I knew it was a Vermeer! She didn't know! Saying it was a Rembrandt and something from before the Italian Renaissance, and she couldn't even recognize a Vermeer right under her nose! Always boasting about art - but she doesn't know anything about it! She's a complete fool. Going on and on about this place - about Enfield and what they did when they were kids, and Richard, Timothy, Laura, and everyone else. These people live in luxury! Always enjoying the best things. You have no idea how annoying it is to listen to someone day after day, year after year, talking about the same things. And all I can say is 'Oh, yes, Mrs. Lanscourt' and 'Really, Mrs. Lanscourt'? Pretending to be interested? It's really annoying - annoying - annoying... and there's nothing to look forward to... and then - a Vermeer original! I saw in the newspaper that a Vermeer sold for over £5,000!"
[2799] "You killed her - in such a cruel way - just for £5,000?" Susan said incredulously.
[2800] "£5,000," said Poirot, "is enough to rent and decorate a tea shop..."
[2801] "At least," she said, "you can understand. It was my only chance. I had to get some money. I wanted to call it 'Palm Trees.' Have the menu printed with little camels on it. Occasionally you can buy really good porcelain – overstock returns – not that pale, utilitarian stuff. I was going to open in a nice area, with people from the upper class. I was thinking of Llandudno or Chester… I'm sure I could make it work."
[2802] She paused, then fell into her own daydreams, "Oak tables – little wicker chairs, with red and white striped cushions…"
[2803] For a moment, the tea shop that would never open seemed more real than the Victorian-era living room of Endicott Manor…
[2804] It was Inspector Morton who broke the spell of her daydreams.
[2805] Miss Gilchrist turned to him politely.
[2806] "Of course," she said, "take me away. I don't want to cause any trouble, I assure you. After all, if I can't have 'Palm Tree,' nothing else matters…”
[2807] He led her out, and Susan's voice still quivered as she said, "I never thought a lady could be a murderer. It's terrible."
[2808] Chapter Twenty-Five
[2809] "But I don't understand the wax flowers," said Rosemond.
[2810] Her large blue eyes fixed on Poirot with a reproachful look.
[2811] They were in Helen's flat in London. Helen was resting on the couch, while Rosemary and Poirot were having afternoon tea together.
[2812] "I can't see any connection between those wax flowers and the whole thing," said Rosemary, "and that emerald table."
[2813] "The emerald table has nothing to do with the whole thing, but the wax flowers are the second mistake made by Miss Gilchrist. She said they looked lovely on the emerald table. You see, madame, she couldn't have seen them there, because the glass cover was broken and put away in the closet before she and the Timotheys arrived at Ennerdale. Therefore, it was only when she impersonated Cora Lanscombe that she could have seen the flowers on the emerald table."
[2814] "She was so stupid, wasn't she?" said Rosemary.
[2815] Poirot wagged his finger in front of her eyes.
[2816] "This tells you the danger of conversation… madam. I deeply believe that if you can lead a person to talk long enough, on any subject, they will eventually betray themselves. Miss Gilchrist is such a case."
[2817] "I'll have to be more careful in the future," Rosemary said thoughtfully.
[2818] She then added happily:
[2819] "Did you know? I'm going to have a baby."
[2820] "Ah-ha! So that's why Harley Street and Regent's Park are what they are."
[2821] "Yes. I was very troubled, you know, and quite surprised – so I had to find a place to think things over."
[2822] "You've said before, I remember, that you're not much of a thinker."
[2823] "Well, it's best not to think. But this time, I had to decide my future. I decided to leave the stage and focus on being a mother."
[2824] "You'll be perfect for that role. I can already imagine the joyful scene."
[2825] Rosamond smiled happily.
[2826] "Yes, it's wonderful. You know, Michael was very happy, and I didn't expect such a reaction from him."
[2827] She paused and then continued:
[2828] "Susan got the table. I think, I already have a little baby—"
[2829] She stopped speaking.
[2830] "Susan's cosmetics business looks promising too," Helen said. "I think she's ready to succeed."
[2831] "Yes, she's born to succeed, just like her uncle," Poirot said.
[2832] "I suppose you mean Richard, don't you?" Poirot said.
[2833] "She certainly doesn't resemble Timothy," Poirot said.
[2834] They laughed.
[2835] "Greg left," Rosamond said, "Susan said he went for a retreat."
[2836] She looked at Poirot with a questioning expression, but he said nothing.
[2837] "I can't understand why he insisted on saying he killed his uncle Richard," said Rosamund. "Do you think it's because of some kind of exhibitionist streak?"
[2838] Poirot returned to the original topic.
[2839] "I received a very friendly letter from Mr. Timothy Abernethie," he said. "He said he was very satisfied with the service I provided for the family."
[2840] "I really find Timothy's uncle quite terrifying."
[2841] "I'll be back with them next week," Helen said, "They seem to have fixed up the garden, but it's still hard to find servants."
[2842] "I guess they must miss that awful Gilchrist," Rosamond said, "But I'm sure she'll end up killing Timothy uncle too. It would be so interesting if she did!"
[2843] "Murder always seems so interesting to you, Madam."
[2844] "Oh! Not really," Rosamond said vaguely, "But I did think it was George who did it," she said cheerfully, "Maybe he'll do it again someday."
[2845] "That would be interesting," Poirot said sarcastically.
[2846] "Yes, wouldn't it?" Rosemary agreed.
[2847] She picked up another cream puff from the plate in front of her.
[2848] Poirot turned to Helen.
[2849] "And you, madam, back to Cyprus?"
[2850] "Yes, in two weeks' time."
[2851] "Then I wish you a pleasant journey."
[2852] He kissed her hand. She accompanied him to the door, leaving Rosamond alone, half-asleep, eating her cream puffs.
[2853] Helen suddenly said:
[2854] "I want you to know, Mr. Poirot, that Richard's inheritance means more to me than it does to any of them."
[2855] "So important, madam?"
[2856] "Yes, you know - there's a child in Cyprus... My husband and I were very much in love, but we never had children, and we were both very sad. After he died, my loneliness was indescribable. After the war, I met someone in London, who was younger than me and married, but his marriage was unhappy. We were together for a while, and then it ended. He went back to Canada, to his wife and children. He had no idea I was pregnant with our child. He wouldn't have wanted it, but it meant so much to me, a middle-aged woman, with such a complicated past. With Richard's money, I can give my so-called nephew a good start in life, educate him properly."
[2857] Poirot kissed her hand again.
[2858] He returned home to find someone sitting in the armchair by the fireplace on the left.
[2859] "Hello, Poirot," Mr. Entwistle said. "I've just come from court. Of course, they found her guilty. But I wouldn't be surprised if she ended up in Broadmoor. She's been off her head since she went to prison, and she's been very happy and very elegant. Most of her time has been spent planning a detailed scheme for a chain of tearooms. Her latest tearoom is called 'Bluebells.' She opened it in Crummer."
[2860] "You must be thinking she's always been a bit odd, but I don't think so."
[2861] "Oh, heavens, no! She was as clear-headed as you and I when she was plotting the murder, and then she coldly carried out the plan. Underneath her dazed exterior, you know, she has a very clever mind."
[2862] Poirot shuddered.
[2863] "I was thinking," he said, "of what Susan Banks had said – that she never imagined a ladylike killer could exist."
[2864] "Why not?" said Mr. Entwistle, "There are all sorts of murderers."
[2865] They fell into silence, Polo reminiscing about the various murderers he had encountered…
